B000SP3DE2|2.0|Francis Coppola's loyal translation of a classic piece of literature merely proves that many times some new wrinkles are necessary to interpret a printed property for the screen. Visually ambitious but thematically dreary, it often seems like a thin excuse to try out some of Hollywood's craziest ideas in wardrobe, set design, hairstyling and film editing. I'll tip my hat to Coppola's dedication to his craft; his demands that the entirety of the film's effects be produced in-camera have given the picture an eerie look entirely its own. When they're working, their influence makes the whole picture seem hazy, uncertain and ghostly, like a foggy, lucid dream. When it all goes wrong, though - and this happens fairly regularly - the illusion shatters and once deathly-serious scenes become downright hysterical. A super-heavyweight cast can do little, if anything, with a script so overcooked and while I'll always welcome a healthy dose of indulgent sex scenes, they really don't seem to have a point here.

I was worried about the fit of the new fan, which was not a problem, because the size, case and blade shape, and mounting holes' positions of the Skythe fan were identical to the fan I was replacing.<br /><br />However, I made an incorrect assumption that all 40mm x 40mm x 10mm fans would be absolutely the same, which is NOT the case. The differences are in RPM and CFM. Skythe fan is very quiet, but it also runs at 60% RPM (3500 vs. 6000) and pushes 64% CFM (4.1 vs. 6.4) of the original fan. I discovered this after I installed Skythe fan and could not tell whether it was running because it was not making any noise and the airflow out of the vent was practically undetectable. The JVC stereo turns the fan on and off as needed, and it was not running when I soldered it on. I had to put the case back together and play a CD twice and then take the case apart while the stereo was plugged in to see whether the fan was indeed running.  It was, but very little air was being pushed by it.<br /><br />I am going to replace it with a fan with specs that are closer to the original fan's specs. I was looking at the Link Depot's ball bearing 40mm x 10mm fan, but several users on newegg complained about the noise, so I have purchased a sleeve bearing ARX Ceradyne-A FD1240-A2012A from xsfans.<br /><br />Overall, I think this is a good quiet fan, but make sure it suits your needs.  If you are looking for more cooling than your current fan , this fan is probably not it.

B00664AM5C|4.0|I recently bought Contagion on blu-ray. Finally after getting the chance to watch it a day later, I decided to write this review while still fresh in my mind.  Contagion very vaguely reminds me of the 1996 medical thriller Outbreak. However, Contagion managed to do something Outbreak never did for me--giving me a nice feeling of suspenseful \\"what's going to happen next\\" dread kind of feeling. I will admit though not really a \\"scary\\" movie, the way everything was portrayed I found more frightening.  Comparisons aside, Contagion is a medical thriller ensemble movie starring Laurence Fishburne, Jude Law, Late Winslet, Matt Damon and featuring the likes of Gwyneth Paltrow. As such, the movie lacks a true central character or storyline. Instead it focuses on three: Laurence Fisburne plays Dr. Leonora Orantes, a CDC worker, Matt Damon as Mitch Ernoff, the husband of Gwynteth Paltrow's character (who is \\"patient zero\\"), and Jude Law as a conspiracy-minded self-serving blogger.  Of the three, I enjoyed the storyline following Matt Damon's character and his daughter the most followed by Laurence Fishburne's. Truthfully I didn't care much for Jude Law's storyline. It wasn't really that it was bad or anything, I just felt it more slowed the movie down than anything. I rather would have had more focus on the medical teams trying to come up with a vaccine, Mitch and his daughter surviving as social order began breaking down around them, or even what Kate Winslet's character did while virtually held hostage in the Chinese village.  The movie follows the respective characters as they continue trying to live and work as everything begins breaking down and panic, rioting, and looting begins to break out across the United States. I thought Contagion did a great job showing the fear among the public and giving off a sense of dread.  The acting was well done and the quality on my HDTV was great so no complaints there. One of the only real complaints I had with the movie was \\"Mitch's\\" reaction to his wife's and six year old son's deaths. One would expect that having two loved ones die so closely with each other, much less one of which is your child, to have a huge impact on you. Instead, other than a scene or two of anger and crying (not even hard crying) he seemed to take their deaths....rather exceedingly well, actually. Just something I noticed.  I think one thing that makes this movie as creepy as it is (if such a word can be used here) is that this is the kind of movie that can actually happen exactly as shown. You don't expect to look out the window one morning to see zombies marching towards your home or a masked serial killer chasing you. But a global pandemic killing millions not only CAN happen but HAS and could very well happen AGAIN.  Don't forget to wash your hands.

B0013LPS6Q|4.0|Suddenly, I find myself reviewing classic releases from the world's most universally recognizable band, having run through every other CD in my collection but the three I'll be consecutively reviewing for U2, and today it's their debut, \\"Boy\\".  Most of the lyrical content the band showcased after \\"Boy\\" centered around  the significance of the place in the universe that man should inhabit with regard to commitment to justice, spiritual enlightenment, and the importance of connections forged on a personal level, but it seems to me that \\"Boy\\" `s focus revolves around the terrifically magical universe of childhood with all of its novelty, cruelty,  exhilaration, loss, and inevitable ascent into a new level of hormonally-driven maturity , and the manner, positive or negative, in which it shapes entry into adulthood. And for every member of U2 at the time that \\"Boy\\" was recorded, there was no such thing as a tenuous connection to it; the oldest member of the band was 20, and it was something still very much captive in recent memory, only a couple of years removed from adolescence. Just look at the titles of some tracks for confirmation; \\"A Day Without Me\\", \\"Out Of Control\\", \\"Stories For Boys\\" , \\"Into The Heart\\", \\"Shadows And Tall Trees\\" , and \\"I Will Follow\\".  Raw and churning and aggressive, textural, haunting and atmospheric, intense, offbeat, and impatient, animated by harmonics rather than any sort of conventional style, Dave (Edge) Evans' guitar work provides a counterpoint for Bono's impassioned delivery of those lyrics, the singer formerly known as Paul Hewson not quite yet in the mature level of his vocal range, and indeed, its universal expanse (in my opinion, at least) wasn't fully developed until \\"War\\". A level, rhythmic insistence developed by Adam Clayton's elementary basslines and Larry Mullen, Jr's percussive flourishes provide steady footing for the distinctive, expressive style both The Edge and Bono were honing even then.  Marking a positive transition into a signed band whose recordings were getting label distribution, U2 presented with a sound already containing some of their signature elements later to make them universally recognizable upon first listen (specifically Edge's subtle, atmospheric, textural , heavily-processed guitar work and Bono's expressive,  charismatic vocal delivery) integrated into tracks that in large part made up for what they lack in technical polish (and in some cases, conventional structure) with remarkable enthusiasm, and a significantly elevated level of intensity. Of all the cuts, only \\"\\"Another Time, Another Place\\" and \\"Twilight\\" ( which I suspect I'll take some heat for) don't really work for me; otherwise, this was a strongly impressive debut, and I can recommend it without hesitation.

The plot was interesting, the pacing (finally, beginning at about the 250 page mark) was excellent, and the writing was mostly clean. I found myself wanting to return to the book a couple days after finishing. That's a good sign.<br /><br />Still, some parts of it were definitely annoying. Like the fact that SO many characters' last names started with B! After awhile, they were hard to keep straight. What's up with that? Also, the lazy use of cliches (\\"dead as a doornail\\" more than once), and the gratuitous use of disturbing violence. Removing the lesser plot would have shortened the book, tightened the pacing and improved the entire manuscript.

At least three talented performers are wasted in the effort.  Anthony Hopkins and Judy Dench are bound by one-dimensional roles that could have conceivably been carried off by non-actors.  Dench's portrayal is proppish and abbreviated, and Hopkins is little more than a foil for the whimsical trans-Atlantic book orders dispatched by Anne Bancroft's character--an aging, eccentric New York spinster who is annoyingly pollyannaish and schmaltzily flip.  There is nothing romantic in her interchange of missives with Hopkins, as any prospective viewer might assume.  She orders classics from London because she can't be bothered to travel several blocks to bookstores or libraries in Gotham (which should have been a book lover's paradise). She engages in childish tantrums--like throwing an imperfect volume against the wall--and fires off sarcastic condemnations of Hopkins's sincere efforts to fill her exacting orders.  Yet, costly, protracted dentalwork, unwarranted arrest, and unexpected eviction scarcely faze her.  If she succumbs to a few moments of real human emotion, she then promptly reassumes her plastic smile and goes skipping along like an over-the-hill That Girl.  The movie itself just keeps going downhill--slowly, tediously, wincingly.  Books are ordered and received.  Gifts of food are ordered and received.  Newsy notes are posted and read.  The years--and the story--drag on.  And, in the end, nothing that Bancroft's character does ever really matters.

That simple phrase sums up the perfect synchronicity of David Fincher's direction, Steven Zaillian's screenplay, and Trent Reznor's score. What kind of a woman asks for permission to kill?<br /><br />By now the plot of TGWTDT is well-known: When Blomkvsit, co-owner of Millennium magazine loses his libel case against corrupt CEO Hans-Erik Wennerstrom (Ulf Friberg), he is given an opportunity at redemption and revenge by Henrik Vanger (Christopher Plummer). It's a good old fashioned murder mystery; Vanger is obsessed with solving the murder of his grandniece Harriet (Moa Garpendal).  And so, slinking out of the public eye, Blomkvist takes the job and moves onto the family's private island.<br /><br />Running parallel to Blomkvist's investigation is the tale of Salander. She is a product of the Swedish system, a ward of the state and abused product of the foster system. It doesn't take long before it becomes clear that Lisbeth is as much a ward as a victim; disaffected, detached, practical, and yes violent, she does what any man would do when facing abuse - she punches it in the face.  But because Lisbeth is a thin, pale girl with piercings all over her body, she is demonized and ostracized.  Lisbeth's greatest nemesis is Nils Bjurman (Yorick van Wageningen), a corpulent, corrupt lawyer who manipulates Lisbeth into performing certain favors on him. And if you know Lisbeth you know this won't end well.<br /><br />Eventually these two plotlines converge.  Lisbeth provides much needed technical wizardry to Blomkvist's old school investigation. They are united by the opportunity to stop a \\"killer of women,\\" a foe Lisbeth can certainly relate to.<br /><br />The crux of TGWTDT is the story of Lisbeth, of Harriet, of all the women who are routinely intimidated and subjugated by men who wield their power without restraint.  Their victims are merely foils, projections for each man's sins, and Lisbeth can't resist the opportunity to help a ghost from 40 years past get redemption.<br /><br />But back to that question.  It means so many things.  On one level, Lisbeth is still asking permission from men.  On the other, she doesn't need to ask anyone for permission for anything. Construction has always been a global industry and is becoming even more so by the day.<br /><br />How useful then, if you are a construction lawyer, to have a clear, detailed and authoritative work of legal reference which not only covers construction law in England and Wales, but provides a comparative study with Australian construction law.<br /><br />Is such a comparison, however, of primary, or merely of secondary importance? If you are, say, a construction lawyer in England and Wales, should you make a point of familiarizing yourself with legal issues which have emerged within the construction industry in Australia? The short answer is `yes,' particularly if your clients' business is global.<br /><br />As author Julian Bailey reminds us, `the common law was exported throughout the British Empire, so that judge-made law, as developed by the English Courts, took root in parts of the Empire including Australia and the many other nations which constituted the Empire.'<br /><br />He adds that a body of law has therefore developed from the various common law countries concerning issues which have direct bearing on construction and engineering projects wherever they are based. Court decisions in construction and engineering cases are regularly made in England on points of law not considered in Australia and vice versa, he points out.<br /><br />It's therefore often helpful, he says, to keep a watchful eye on developments in both jurisdictions, so as to give an indication of how particular legal issues may be, or are likely to be resolved.<br /><br />One example of such an issue is the problem of the legal identity of a company and whether the identity of parent company and its subsidiaries is distinct. There are occasions, warns Bailey, 'when the \\"corporate veil\\" will be pierced so that a parent company may be held liable for the conduct of its subsidiary, or may recover a loss suffered by its subsidiary.' These points and related matters are discussed in detail typical of the author's clear, penetrating, insightful and thorough approach.<br /><br />The result is a meticulously footnoted three volume work of reference logically laid out, readable and easy to use, The index at the back of each volume umbers on average, over 60 pages and there is of course, a full and detailed table of contents, Research resources abound, including at least 200 pages in the first volume alone, of tables of cases, statutes and statutory instruments.<br /><br />Lord Justice Jackson, in the Foreword, calls this three volume work a `tour-de-force' and we cannot but agree. The work takes a thorough, scholarly, yet readable and practical approach to an extremely wide ranging, dynamic and continually changing area of law. B005LAIGN2|3.0|Red Tails is a captivating film that delves into the issue of racial inequality and segregation in the military during World War II. This movie is based on true events that transpired during the height of the Jim Crow era, and it is a story that George Lucas (creator of Star Wars) felt needed to be shared in full cinematic capacity. The story revolves around the 332nd Tuskegee Fighter Group, which was comprised of elite African American pilots. The main theme of this film is based on the fact that the United States government did not have faith in African American pilots and concluded that they were mentally and genetically inferior to whites because of biased case studies that took place in 1925. Therefore, they deduced that the Airmen could not complete combat missions, but when the U.S. and European powers had their backs against the wall because the Nazi's were gaining the upper hand, it was up to the Tuskegee Fighter Group to help win the war against tyranny.<br /><br />Red Tails had great subplots, which will keep you engaged throughout the film, plus the characters are well developed and emotional. You will get a sense of being right in the middle of the action because the CGI used was impeccable. Overall, this was a historically entertaining piece of cinematography that any fan of these types of movies will enjoy. This is a must watch film because of its historical value, plus the fact that Terrance Howard and Cuba Gooding Jr. star in the film is a plus since they always give outstanding performances.<br /><br />Red Tails receives Three STARS!!!!!

1594203334|5.0|I was in Bali, Indonesia several years ago, staying at a hotel that provided free breakfast to its guests.  There was a French family (with five children: ages 1-12, approximately)staying there, and each morning, the waitresses brought out one fruit smoothie at a time, one plate of food at a time, etc.  Charming hotel; take-your-time service.  Anyway, I was captivated by this family because I noticed how they were all talking to each other, enjoying each other's company - and waiting patiently for their food.  There was no fighting, whining, or anything like that for the entire week.  I wondered at the time, was this a French thing - or just this particular French family?  Then, I forgot about that until I started reading this book last week!  The negative reviews on here for this book are startling to me.  Some just sound as if they have a personal axe to grind while others sound self-righteous (e.g. \\"I'm such a perfect American parent that I'm offended anyone would think I need to read this book\\").  The author's point is not to attack or offend; she's simply discussing her personal journey in discovering what works for her and her children.  Druckerman is mostly mocking herself, and she very clearly states that not all French parents do x, not all American parents do y, etc. - and that she is not focusing on the entire French population.  One of her points - albeit implicit at times - is that there is an overall difference between middle to upper-middle class families in Paris, compared to those in New York City with regard to child rearing.  I am nearly finished with this book and have found it one of the most interesting, readable, humorous books I've read in the last few months(I'm an avid reader), and I don't even have kids!  I am a teacher, so I do see first-hand, everyday episodes of impatience and children who demand that their needs be met right away.  Yes, there are many American parents who parent well and work to mitigate this poor type of behavior, butthis is not commonplace in our society, and if we're all really honest with  ourselves (been out to dinner lately when other families with children are also in the restaurant?), we can recognize this.  Anyway, if you want to laugh and are interested in opening your minds up to a different culture - and you're responsible for shaping young people in some regard - order this book right now!  It's fascinating and thought-provoking!

1400052181|3.0|This is a fascinating look at the story of Henrietta Lacks, a poor woman who died in the early 1950s from a particular virulent form of cervical cancer.  At the time scientists were trying to find human cells that would stay alive in a test tube beyond just a day or so, so they could conduct research. Henrietta had this cancer along with syphilis (and possibly gonorrhea) - essentially a perfect storm of ingredients. When they scraped her cancer cells and put them in test tubes not only did they not die, they replicated at an astonishing rate. Today her cells are used all over the world and have been used to help countless patients, and have provided medical advancements that have eased the pain and help cure the sick. It's been a boon to mankind.<br /><br /> Not being in the medical field, I had not heard of HeLa cells before. It was fascinating to find out about them. Indeed the ethical questions raised are of great interest - whether or not we can take someone's cells without permission and profit from them. Remember that back when Henrietta's cells were taken, everyone else's were as well - there weren't the issues of privacy and informed consent that we have today.<br /><br />Henrietta's cells were not taken from her because she was black, and all the evidence pointed to the fact that she got the standard medical care of the day. By focusing on the racial issue I think it's misleading and ultimately dangerous because by doing so, people can then dismiss real examples of racial prejudice by not taking them seriously.<br /><br /> No doubt it's a terrible tragedy that the Lacks family has inadequate medical care today - but I think that's a tragedy for anyone without it.<br /><br />I think this book would make for a great book club discussion. There are several doctors in my book club and we had a really interesting time talking about all this.

0307352145|5.0|For those who dread, rather than anticipate, parties, crowds and other social events, \\"Quiet\\" will take you from \\"What's wrong with me?\\" to recognizing the complex social roles that the \\"shy\\" or \\"quiet\\" personalities play.  Like me, you might end the book wondering why the hyper-social, extroverted kids aren't the ones sent to the Resource Room; perhaps their behavior could be modified to be less loud, more aware...?    Susan Cain's premise is that introverts have always gotten the message that there's something about them that needs fixing - or they're failing to meet certain social performance standards. But \\"Quiet\\" suggests that while Americans (and the world) enjoy outstanding benefits from quiet people, we also pay a high price for under-valuing them. (From the book-- how different Bill Clinton would be if he'd been pressured to conform to a \\"Bill Gates\\" personality or Bill Gates had been required to be more like Bill Clinton!) One of the best aspects of this book is how Cain zings in on introvert-specific traits (the ones even introverts view as quirky or fringy or even disordered) and demonstrates how absolutely critical they are to our progress in the arts and sciences.    \\"Quiet\\" is an especially timely book with the diagnosis of Asperger's and debilitating shyness and other spectrum \\"disorders\\" on the rise (and being behaviorally modified). It's naturally written and authoritative but there's no need (much) to buzz over scientific jargon; it's the kind of writing that makes me feel smart.:-)  Cain makes a solid, entertaining argument that the introverted personality that we've all been conditioned to be concerned about, would be better off celebrated and cultivated. As an audio book, another 5 stars.

After seeing Phantom staged at the RAH on bluray my hopes and dreams have been almost fully realized.  Pros:  -It heals the hurts caused by the soul-less Joel Schumacher version.  -The casting is fantastic!  The Phantom is great, but Christine and Raoul shine in their roles.  -The wide shots are great, capturing the unique digital backdrops and staging.  -Seeing a cast of over 100 performing Phantom is wonderful!  -The little additions to the music (using a full orchestra) are great.  -It is a great production, performed remarkably well, in a timeless setting.  -The curtain calls, and all the bonus stuff afterward, is wonderful Phan service!  Cons:  -It is still a stage production, so the visible presence of microphones and stage makeup will likely jar some viewers.  -The closer the camera zooms in, the more the digital backdrops look pixelated.  -Despite the fact that it is Phantom, it is not staged as you would see it done on Broadway.  Large props (the elephant) are left out entirely, and the chandelier does not actually fall.  As long as you expect a new interpretation of some of these things, it is not a con.
